</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1129693374"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Eastern_Orthodox_Church_sidebar" title="Template:Eastern Orthodox Church sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Eastern_Orthodox_Church_sidebar" title="Template talk:Eastern Orthodox Church sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Eastern_Orthodox_Church_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Maximus the Confessor</b> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el">Μάξιμος ὁ <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%BF%CE%BC%CE%BF%CE%BB%CE%BF%CE%B3%CE%B7%CF%84%CE%AE%CF%82" class="extiw" title="wikt:ομολογητής">Ὁμολογητής</a></span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Greek" title="Romanization of Greek">romanized</a>:&#160;</small><span title="Greek-language romanization"><i lang="el-Latn">Maximos ho Homologētēs</i></span>), also spelled <b>Maximos</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> otherwise known as <b>Maximus the Theologian</b> and <b>Maximus of Constantinople</b> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;580</span> – 13 August 662), was a <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christian</a> <a href="/wiki/Monk" title="Monk">monk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theologian" class="mw-redirect" title="Theologian">theologian</a>, and scholar.
</p><p>In his early life, Maximus was a civil servant, and an aide to the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Emperor" class="mw-redirect" title="Byzantine Emperor">Byzantine Emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Heraclius" title="Heraclius">Heraclius</a>. He gave up this life in the political sphere to enter the monastic life. Maximus had studied diverse schools of philosophy, and certainly what was common for his time, the Platonic dialogues, the works of Aristotle, and numerous later Platonic commentators on Aristotle and Plato, like <a href="/wiki/Plotinus" title="Plotinus">Plotinus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Porphyry_(philosopher)" title="Porphyry (philosopher)">Porphyry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iamblichus" title="Iamblichus">Iamblichus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Proclus" title="Proclus">Proclus</a>. When one of his friends began espousing the <a href="/wiki/Christology" title="Christology">Christological</a> position known as <a href="/wiki/Monothelitism" title="Monothelitism">Monothelitism</a>, Maximus was drawn into the controversy, in which he supported an interpretation of the <a href="/wiki/Chalcedonian" class="mw-redirect" title="Chalcedonian">Chalcedonian</a> formula on the basis of which it was asserted that <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a> had both a human and a divine <a href="/wiki/Will_(philosophy)" title="Will (philosophy)">will</a>. Maximus is <a href="/wiki/Veneration" title="Veneration">venerated</a> in both the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox</a> Churches. He was eventually persecuted for his Christological positions; following a trial, his tongue and right hand were mutilated.
</p><p>He was then exiled and died on 13 August 662, in <a href="/wiki/Tsageri" title="Tsageri">Tsageri</a> in present-day <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>. However, his theology was upheld by the <a href="/wiki/Third_Council_of_Constantinople" title="Third Council of Constantinople">Third Council of Constantinople</a> and he was venerated as a saint soon after his death. His title of "<a href="/wiki/Confessor_of_the_Faith" title="Confessor of the Faith">Confessor</a>" means that he suffered for the Christian faith, but was not directly <a href="/wiki/Martyr" title="Martyr">martyred</a>. His feast day is 13 August in the Western Church and 21 January in the Byzantine Church.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life">Life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Maximus_the_Confessor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Maximus_the_Confessor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Very little is known about the details of Maximus' life prior to his involvement in the theological and political conflicts of the Monothelite controversy.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Numerous Maximian scholars call substantial portions of the Maronite biography into question, including Maximus' birth in Palestine, which was a common seventh century trope to discredit an opponent. Moreover, the exceptional education Maximus evidently received could not have been had in any other part of the Byzantine Empire during that time except for Constantinople, and possibly Caesarea and Alexandria. It is also very unlikely that anyone of low social birth, as the <a href="/wiki/Maronite_Church" title="Maronite Church">Maronite</a> biography describes Maximus, could have ascended by the age of thirty to be the <a href="/wiki/Protasekretis" title="Protasekretis">Protoasekretis</a> of the Emperor Heraclius, one of the most powerful positions in the Empire. It is more likely that Maximus was born of an aristocratic family and received an unparalleled education in philosophy, mathematics, astronomy, etc. It is true, however, that Maximus did not study rhetoric as he himself notes in the prologue to his Earlier Ambigua to John,<sup id="cite_ref-Constas_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Constas-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> to which his lack of high stylistic by Byzantine standards attests. Nevertheless, for reasons not explained in the few autobiographical details to be gleaned from his texts, Maximus left public life and took <a href="/wiki/Monastic_vows" class="mw-redirect" title="Monastic vows">monastic vows</a> at the monastery of <a href="/wiki/Philippicus_(comes_excubitorum)" title="Philippicus (comes excubitorum)">Philippicus</a> in <a href="/wiki/%C3%9Csk%C3%BCdar" title="Üsküdar">Chrysopolis</a>, a city across the <a href="/wiki/Bosporus" title="Bosporus">Bosporus</a> from Constantinople. Maximus was elevated to the position of <a href="/wiki/Hegumen" title="Hegumen">abbot</a> of the monastery.<sup id="cite_ref-cathency_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cathency-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>When the <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Persians</a> conquered <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>, Maximus was forced to flee to a monastery near <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthage</a>. It was there that he came under the tutelage of <a href="/wiki/Sophronius_of_Jerusalem" title="Sophronius of Jerusalem">Saint Sophronius</a>, and began studying in detail with him the <a href="/wiki/Christology" title="Christology">Christological</a> writings of <a href="/wiki/Gregory_of_Nazianzus" title="Gregory of Nazianzus">Gregory of Nazianzus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pseudo-Dionysius_the_Areopagite" title="Pseudo-Dionysius the Areopagite">Pseudo-Dionysius the Areopagite</a>. According to I P Sheldon Williams his achievement was to set these doctrines into a framework of Aristotelian logic, which both suited the temper of the times and made them less liable to misinterpretation.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Maximus continued his career as a theological and spiritual writer during his lengthy stay in Carthage.<sup id="cite_ref-Berthold_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berthold-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Maximus was also held in high esteem by the <a href="/wiki/Exarch_of_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="Exarch of Africa">exarch</a> <a href="/wiki/Gregory_the_Patrician" title="Gregory the Patrician">Gregory</a> and the eparch <a href="/wiki/George_(eparch)" title="George (eparch)">George</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Involvement_in_Monothelite_controversy">Involvement in Monothelite controversy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Maximus_the_Confessor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Involvement in Monothelite controversy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<figure class="mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Hexagram-Constans_II_and_Constantine_IV-sb0995.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Hexagram-Constans_II_and_Constantine_IV-sb0995.jpg/300px-Hexagram-Constans_II_and_Constantine_IV-sb0995.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="150" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Hexagram-Constans_II_and_Constantine_IV-sb0995.jpg/450px-Hexagram-Constans_II_and_Constantine_IV-sb0995.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5f/Hexagram-Constans_II_and_Constantine_IV-sb0995.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="299" /></a><figcaption>A silver <i><a href="/wiki/Hexagram_(currency)" title="Hexagram (currency)">hexagramma</a></i> showing Constans II with his son. Constans II supported <a href="/wiki/Monothelitism" title="Monothelitism">Monothelitism</a>, and had Maximus exiled for his refusal to agree to Monothelite teachings.</figcaption></figure>
<p>While Maximus was in Carthage, a controversy broke out regarding how to understand the interaction between the human and divine natures within the <a href="/wiki/Incarnation_(Christianity)" title="Incarnation (Christianity)">person</a> of <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a>. This Christological debate was the latest development in disagreements that began following the <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Nicaea" title="First Council of Nicaea">First Council of Nicaea</a> in 325, and were intensified following the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Chalcedon" title="Council of Chalcedon">Council of Chalcedon</a> in 451. The <a href="/wiki/Monothelite" class="mw-redirect" title="Monothelite">Monothelite</a> position was developed as a compromise between the <a href="/wiki/Dyophysite" class="mw-redirect" title="Dyophysite">dyophysitists</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Miaphysitism" title="Miaphysitism">miaphysists</a>, who believed dyophysitism is conceptually indistinguishable from <a href="/wiki/Nestorianism" title="Nestorianism">Nestorianism</a>. The Monothelites adhered to the <a href="/wiki/Chalcedonian" class="mw-redirect" title="Chalcedonian">Chalcedonian</a> definition of the <a href="/wiki/Hypostatic_union" title="Hypostatic union">hypostatic union</a>: that two natures, one divine and one human, were united in the person of Christ. However, they went on to say that Christ had only a divine will and no human will (Monothelite is derived from the Greek for "one will").
</p><p>The Monothelite position was promulgated by <a href="/wiki/Patriarch_Sergius_I_of_Constantinople" class="mw-redirect" title="Patriarch Sergius I of Constantinople">Patriarch Sergius I of Constantinople</a> and by Maximus' friend and successor as the Abbot of Chrysopolis, <a href="/wiki/Patriarch_Pyrrhus_of_Constantinople" class="mw-redirect" title="Patriarch Pyrrhus of Constantinople">Pyrrhus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Following the death of Sergius in 638, Pyrrhus succeeded him as Patriarch, but was shortly deposed owing to political circumstances. During Pyrrhus' exile from Constantinople, Maximus and the deposed Patriarch held a public debate on the issue of Monothelitism. In the debate, which was held in the presence of many North African bishops, Maximus took the position that Jesus possessed both a human and a divine will. The result of the debate was that Pyrrhus admitted the error of the Monothelite position, and Maximus accompanied him to Rome in 645.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Maximus may have remained in Rome, because he was present when the newly elected <a href="/wiki/Pope_Martin_I" title="Pope Martin I">Pope Martin I</a> convened the <a href="/wiki/Lateran_Council_of_649" title="Lateran Council of 649">Lateran Council of 649</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_St._John_Lateran" class="mw-redirect" title="Basilica of St. John Lateran">Lateran Basilica</a> in Rome.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The 105 bishops present condemned Monothelitism in the official acts of the synod, which some believe may have been written by Maximus.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> It was in Rome that Pope Martin and Maximus were arrested in 653 under orders from <a href="/wiki/Constans_II_(Byzantine_Empire)" class="mw-redirect" title="Constans II (Byzantine Empire)">Constans II</a>, who supported the Monothelite doctrine. Pope Martin was condemned without a trial, and died before he could be sent to the Imperial Capital.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trial_and_exile">Trial and exile</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Maximus_the_Confessor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Trial and exile">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Maximus' refusal to accept Monothelitism caused him to be brought to the imperial capital of <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> to be tried as a <a href="/wiki/Heresy" title="Heresy">heretic</a> in 658. In Constantinople, Monothelitism had gained the favor of both the Emperor and the Patriarch of Constantinople. Maximus stood behind the Dyothelite position and was sent back into exile for four more years. During his trial he was accused of aiding the Muslim conquests <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Egypt" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim conquest of Egypt">in Egypt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_the_Maghreb" title="Muslim conquest of the Maghreb">North Africa</a>, which he rejected as slander.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:44-manasses-chronicle.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/44-manasses-chronicle.jpg/220px-44-manasses-chronicle.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="303" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/44-manasses-chronicle.jpg/330px-44-manasses-chronicle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/44-manasses-chronicle.jpg/440px-44-manasses-chronicle.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2351" data-file-height="3242" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Constans_II" title="Constans II">Constans II</a> (left) having Maximus beaten for refusing to accept <a href="/wiki/Monothelitism" title="Monothelitism">Monothelitism</a>. Miniature from the 12th century <a href="/wiki/Manasses_Chronicle" class="mw-redirect" title="Manasses Chronicle">Manasses Chronicle</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>In 662, Maximus was placed on trial once more, and was once more convicted of heresy. Following the trial Maximus was tortured, having his tongue cut out, so he could no longer speak his rebellion, and his right hand cut off, so that he could no longer write letters.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Maximus was then exiled to the <a href="/wiki/Lazica" title="Lazica">Lazica</a> or <a href="/wiki/Colchis" title="Colchis">Colchis</a> region of modern-day <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a> and was cast in the fortress of Schemarum, perhaps Muris-Tsikhe near the modern town of <a href="/wiki/Tsageri" title="Tsageri">Tsageri</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> He died soon thereafter, on 13 August 662.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The events of the trials of Maximus were recorded by <a href="/wiki/Anastasius_Bibliothecarius" title="Anastasius Bibliothecarius">Anastasius Bibliothecarius</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Maximus_the_Confessor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Maksim_ispovednik.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Maksim_ispovednik.jpg/285px-Maksim_ispovednik.jpg" decoding="async" width="285" height="359" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Maksim_ispovednik.jpg/428px-Maksim_ispovednik.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Maksim_ispovednik.jpg/570px-Maksim_ispovednik.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1400" data-file-height="1764" /></a><figcaption><i>Maximus the Confessor and His Miracles</i>. An early 17th-century <a href="/wiki/Stroganov_school" class="mw-redirect" title="Stroganov school">Stroganov school</a> icon from <a href="/wiki/Solvychegodsk" title="Solvychegodsk">Solvychegodsk</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Along with Pope Martin I, Maximus was vindicated by the <a href="/wiki/Third_Council_of_Constantinople" title="Third Council of Constantinople">Third Council of Constantinople</a> (the Sixth <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecumenical Council">Ecumenical Council</a>, 680–681), which declared that <a href="/wiki/Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Christ">Christ</a> possessed both a human and a divine will. With this declaration Monothelitism became heresy, and Maximus was posthumously declared innocent.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Maximus is among those Christians who were venerated as <a href="/wiki/Saint" title="Saint">saints</a> shortly after their deaths. The vindication of Maximus' theological position made him extremely popular within a generation after his death, and his cause was aided by the accounts of miracles at his tomb.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Maximus is one of the last men to be recognized by both the Orthodox and Catholic Churches as a Father of the Church. In the encyclical <i>Spe Salvi</i> (2007), Pope Benedict XVI called Maximus 'the great Greek doctor of the Church', although it is not clear if the Pontiff intended to nominate Maximus 'Doctor of the Church' or to say that he already was one.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theology">Theology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Maximus_the_Confessor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Theology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>As a student of <a href="/wiki/Pseudo-Dionysius" class="mw-redirect" title="Pseudo-Dionysius">Pseudo-Dionysius</a>, Maximus was one of many Christian theologians who preserved and interpreted the earlier <a href="/wiki/Neo-Platonic" class="mw-redirect" title="Neo-Platonic">Neo-Platonic</a> philosophy, including the thought of such figures as <a href="/wiki/Plotinus" title="Plotinus">Plotinus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Proclus" title="Proclus">Proclus</a>. Maximus' work on Pseudo-Dionysius the Areopagite was continued by <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Scotus_Eriugena" class="mw-redirect" title="Johannes Scotus Eriugena">John Scotus Eriugena</a> at the request of <a href="/wiki/Charles_the_Bald" title="Charles the Bald">Charles the Bald</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CE_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CE-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Platonic influence on Maximus' thought can be seen most clearly in his <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_anthropology" title="Philosophical anthropology">theological anthropology</a>. Here, Maximus adopted the Platonic model of <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism#Ideas" title="Neoplatonism">exitus-reditus</a> (exit and return), teaching that humanity was made in the <a href="/wiki/Imago_Dei" class="mw-redirect" title="Imago Dei">image of God</a>, and the purpose of salvation is to restore us to unity with God.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> This emphasis on <a href="/wiki/Divinization_(Christian)" title="Divinization (Christian)">divinization</a> or <i><a href="/wiki/Theosis_(Eastern_Orthodox_theology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Theosis (Eastern Orthodox theology)">theosis</a></i> helped secure Maximus' place in Eastern theology, as these concepts have always held an important place in Eastern Christianity.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Christologically Maximus insisted on a strict <a href="/wiki/Dyophysitism" title="Dyophysitism">dyophysitism</a>, which can be seen as a corollary of the emphasis on <i>theosis</i>. In terms of salvation, humanity is intended to be fully united with God. This is possible for Maximus because God was first fully united with humanity in the incarnation.<sup id="cite_ref-CE_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CE-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> If Christ did not become fully human (if, for example, he only had a divine and not a human will), then salvation was no longer possible, as humanity could not become fully divine.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, in his works Maximus the Confessor argued the unconditionality of the divine incarnation.<sup id="cite_ref-Radosavljević_1975_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Radosavljević_1975-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Regarding salvation, Maximus, like <a href="/wiki/Origen" title="Origen">Origen</a> and <a href="/wiki/St._Gregory_of_Nyssa" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Gregory of Nyssa">St. Gregory of Nyssa</a>, has been described as a proponent of <i><a href="/wiki/Apocatastasis" title="Apocatastasis">apocatastasis</a></i> or <a href="/wiki/Universal_reconciliation" class="mw-redirect" title="Universal reconciliation">universal reconciliation</a>, the idea that all rational souls will eventually be redeemed.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> While this claim has been disputed,<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> others have argued that Maximus shared this belief in <a href="/wiki/Universal_reconciliation" class="mw-redirect" title="Universal reconciliation">universal reconciliation</a> with his most spiritually mature students.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reception">Reception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Maximus_the_Confessor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Reception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Christianity" title="Eastern Christianity">Eastern Christianity</a>, Maximus has always been influential.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> A number of his works are included in the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Philokalia" title="Philokalia">Philokalia</a></i>, a collection of some of the most influential <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Christian</a> writers.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Writings">Writings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Maximus_the_Confessor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Writings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<p><b>Attributed texts</b>
</p>
<ul><li><i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scholia_(Maximus_the_Confessor)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Scholia (Maximus the Confessor) (page does not exist)">Scholia</a></i> – commentary on the earlier writings of Pseudo-Dionysius. The original edition in Latin of Balthasar Corderius (Antwerp 1634) attributes all of the Scholia to Maximus, but the authorship has been questioned with <a href="/wiki/Hans_Urs_von_Balthasar" title="Hans Urs von Balthasar">Hans Urs von Balthasar</a> (1940, 1961) attributing some of the Scholia to <a href="/wiki/John_of_Scythopolis" title="John of Scythopolis">John of Scythopolis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Life_of_the_Virgin_(Biography)" class="mw-redirect" title="Life of the Virgin (Biography)">Life of the Virgin</a></i> – earliest complete biography of Mary, the mother of Jesus.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> This is an attributed work and now believed not to be by Maximus the Confessor. Jankowiak and Booth argue that "none of Maximus' characteristic preoccupations appear in the <i>Life</i>, and in turn none of the <i>Life' </i>s central themes appear in the fleeting Marian reflections contained within his genuine corpus". They also write that there is no Greek manuscript witnessing the text, no evidence that any key thinkers who draw on Maximus were aware of the <i>Life' </i>s existence and that no record of the <i>Life</i> as a work exists prior to the second half of the tenth century.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>

<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><cite id="CITEREFPetruzzello2023" class="citation web cs1">Petruzzello, Melissa (2023). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Saint-Maximus-the-Confessor">"St. Maximus the Confessor"</a>. <i>Britannica</i>. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">29 June</span> 2023</span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Britannica&amp;rft.atitle=St.+Maximus+the+Confessor&amp;rft.date=2023&amp;rft.aulast=Petruzzello&amp;rft.aufirst=Melissa&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.britannica.com%2Fbiography%2FSaint-Maximus-the-Confessor&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMaximus+the+Confessor" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The following account is based on the lengthy tenth-century biography catalogued as BHG 1234 and printed in Migne's Patrologia Graeca (90, 68A1-109B9). In recent years, however, this account has been called into question on the basis of new scholarly research. The author, or rather compiler, of BHG 1234 turns out to have used one of the biographies of Theodore the Studite (BHG 1755) to fill the gaps in the information he had on Maximus (See W. Lackner, Zu Quellen und Datierung der Maximosvita (BHG<sup>3</sup> 1234), in Analecta Bollandiana 85 [1967], p. 285-316). The information the compiler of BHG 1234 did have he drew from the passions extant at the time, in which nothing is said about Maximus' early years (See B. Roosen, Maximi Confessoris Vitae et Passiones Graecae. The Development of a Hagiographic Dossier, in Byzantion 80 [2010], forthcoming). On the basis of mostly internal evidence from Maximus' writings, C. Boudignon advocates a Palestinian birth for Maximus instead (See C. Boudignon, Maxime le Confesseur était-il constantinopolitain?, in B. Janssens – B. Roosen – P. Van Deun [ed.], Philomathestatos. Studies in Greek and Byzantine Texts Presented to Jacques Noret for his Sixty-Fifth Birthday [= Orientalia Lovaniensia Analecta 137], Leuven – Paris – Dudley, MA, 2004, p. 11-43; and id., Le pouvoir de l'anathème ou Maxime le Confesseur et les moines palestiniens du VIIe siècle, in A. Camplani – G. Filoramo, Foundations of Power and  Conflicts of Authority in Late-Antique Monasticism. Proceedings of the International Seminar, Turin, 2–4 December 2004 [= Orientalia Lovaniensia Analecta, 157], Leuven – Paris – Dudley, MA, 2007, p. 245-274). If this is true, it confirms the value of the Maronite biography, even though it is clearly anti-Maximian.</span>
</li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="External_links">External links</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Maximus_the_Confessor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: External links">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1134653256">.mw-parser-output .side-box{margin:4px 0;box-sizing:border-box;border:1px solid #aaa;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em;background-color:#f9f9f9;display:flow-root}.mw-parser-output .side-box-abovebelow,.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{padding:0.25em 0.9em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-image{padding:2px 0 2px 0.9em;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-imageright{padding:2px 0.9em 2px 0;text-align:center}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .side-box-flex{display:flex;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{flex:1}}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .side-box{width:238px}.mw-parser-output .side-box-right{clear:right;float:right;margin-left:1em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-left{margin-right:1em}}</style><div class="side-box side-box-right plainlinks sistersitebox"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1126788409">
